<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_22065030">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/22065030" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/92397-brittany" class="left imgcol" title="Brittany"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1191511794p2/92397.jpg" alt="Brittany" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/22065030?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Feb 28, 2012</a>

      <a href="/user/show/92397-brittany" class="user" title="Brittany">Brittany</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/538321.Last_Chance_to_See" title="Hardcover 9780517582152" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/92397-brittany?shelf=sciencewriting" class="actionLinkLite">sciencewriting</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/92397-brittany?shelf=wildlife" class="actionLinkLite">wildlife</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/92397-brittany?shelf=favorites" class="actionLinkLite">favorites</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer22065030">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12109964673666444556">I love Douglas Adams's science fiction. Just look at my bookshelves. So it's as a firm fan that I say: Douglas Adams was wasted--<em>wasted</em>--on science fiction.<br /><br />The man is obviously a science writer.<br /><br />His science fiction was always good. Clearly. But none of it sings like <a title="Last Chance to See by Douglas Adams" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/8696.Last_Chance_to_See">Last Chance to See</a>. This book is a passionate, loving, critical look at the human species and the influence we've had on our planet-mates. It chronicles the decline, and impending loss, of some wonderful, charismatic vertebrates. It t</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText12109964673666444556">I love Douglas Adams's science fiction. Just look at my bookshelves. So it's as a firm fan that I say: Douglas Adams was wasted--<em>wasted</em>--on science fiction.<br /><br />The man is obviously a science writer.<br /><br />His science fiction was always good. Clearly. But none of it sings like <a title="Last Chance to See by Douglas Adams" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/8696.Last_Chance_to_See">Last Chance to See</a>. This book is a passionate, loving, critical look at the human species and the influence we've had on our planet-mates. It chronicles the decline, and impending loss, of some wonderful, charismatic vertebrates. It takes us to task for the degradation of the planet, and makes us feel the tragic loss of our heritage, but it never depresses. It bounces up from the darkest moments with Adams's trademark dark humor. Of course, that humor has the effect of throwing all the rest into sharp relief, highlighting the tragedy and wounding your heart. That's what makes it such a powerful book, and one everyone should read. <br /><br />The beauty is that it's also smooth and lucid enough that everyone <em>can</em> read it. He never preaches, and the book always keeps the tone of a story told around a campfire, among friends.<br /><br />If this doesn't inspire anyone who reads it to care just a little bit more about the non-human, but still precious, species that inhabit Earth, then I will give up trying to save them tomorrow. But at the same time, Adams's courage, compassion, humility, and humor make a compelling case for humanity's continued existence as a species.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="12109964673666444556">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review nosyndicate" id="review_381571932">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/381571932" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/6969151-veeral" class="left imgcol" title="Veeral"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1344326461p2/6969151.jpg" alt="Veeral" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/381571932?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Aug 01, 2015</a>

      <a href="/user/show/6969151-veeral" class="user" title="Veeral">Veeral</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>



        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/6969151-veeral?shelf=non-fiction" class="actionLinkLite">non-fiction</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/6969151-veeral?shelf=adventure" class="actionLinkLite">adventure</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/6969151-veeral?shelf=funny" class="actionLinkLite">funny</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/6969151-veeral?shelf=travelogue" class="actionLinkLite">travelogue</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/6969151-veeral?shelf=favorites-of-2012" class="actionLinkLite">favorites-of-2012</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/6969151-veeral?shelf=read-in-2012" class="actionLinkLite">read-in-2012</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/6969151-veeral?shelf=exploration" class="actionLinkLite">exploration</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer381571932">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8300421097395478162"><br />Douglas Adams went around the globe along with zoologist Mark Carwardine in search of various species of animals and birds which were on the verge of extinction in 1985 (when this book was written). My interest was piqued on the thought that if these species were considered endangered in 1985, what would be their current status as of 2012? Well I did some research <em>(I mean I Googled it. But not in an amateurish way, I tried hard enough until I got bored, i.e. after 15 minutes!)</em><br /><br />And one thing that</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText8300421097395478162"><br />Douglas Adams went around the globe along with zoologist Mark Carwardine in search of various species of animals and birds which were on the verge of extinction in 1985 (when this book was written). My interest was piqued on the thought that if these species were considered endangered in 1985, what would be their current status as of 2012? Well I did some research <em>(I mean I Googled it. But not in an amateurish way, I tried hard enough until I got bored, i.e. after 15 minutes!)</em><br /><br />And one thing that I cannot understand is why even some educated people consider the extinction of various species a natural phenomenon! Just say these words in front of a well educated crowd and you almost know what answer you are going to get. Global warming? Pah. Government conspiracy. Extinction of various species? It is not a new thing. It’s just a cycle. But what if someone told you that the rate of extinction has increased exponentially in the last 50 years or so? And just because Al Gore supports the campaign against global warming doesn’t make it a conspiracy. <br /><br />Anyway, let’s just look at the comparison of the species population which Adams saw in 1985 compared to the current year 2012. <br /><br /><strong>Aye Aye(Madagascar) - unknown pop. (1985) – Fortunately they are more widespread than previously thought (2012)<br /><br />Northern White Rhino (Zaire- Africa) – 22 nos. (1985) – Extinct (2012) (Only 7 remain in captivity)<br /><br />Mountain Gorrillas (Zaire- Africa) – 280 nos. (1985) – 790 nos. (2012) (But endangered due to activities like deforestation and poaching)<br /><br />Kakapo (New Zealand)– 40 nos. (1985) – 126 nos. (2012) <br /><br />Yangtze River Dolphin aka Baiji (China) – 200 nos. (1985) – Extinct (2012)<br /><br />The Komodo dragon (Indonesia)– 5000 nos. (350 females) (1985) – 4000-5000 nos. (2012)<br /><br />Finless porpoise (Yangtze River, China)– 400 nos. (1985) – less than 400 nos. (2012)<br /><br />The Rodrigues fruitbat (Mauritius) – 100 nos. (1985) – 3000 nos. and rising (2012) <br /><br />Mauritius kestrel (Mauritius) - 100 nos. (1985) – 3000 nos. and rising (2012)<br /><br />Echo Parakeet (Mauritius) – 15 nos. (1985) – 130 nos. (2012)<br /><br />Pink pigeons (Mauritius) – 200 nos. (1985) – 350 nos. (2012)</strong><br /><br />Well, not everyone made it. And those who are faring better comparatively are still considered endangered if not critically endangered. And these are among the lucky few who were saved because of the much required publicity received from various sources including, I think, this book.<br /><br />Apart from these species, Adams also saw some of the rarest species of flora. In his own words:<br /><br /><em>I knew that the palm tree was called Beverly because Wendy told me that was what she had christened it. It was a bottle palm, so called because it is shaped like a Chianti bottle, and it was one of the eight that remain on Round Island, the only eight wild ones in the world.<br /><br />Or that the Hyophorbe amarfcaulis (a palm tree so rare that it doesn't have any name other than its scientific one) standing in the Curepipe Botanic Gardens in Mauritius is the only one of its kind in existence? (The tree was only discovered by chance while the ground on which it stands was being cleared in order to construct the Botanic Gardens. It was about to be cut down.)</em><br /><br />But a skeptic would still ask that why is it only and only our (human beings') fault that earth’s ecology is crumbling? Well, Adams countered it perfectly:<br /><br /><strong>The great thing about being the only species that makes a distinction between right and wrong is that we can make up the rules for ourselves as we go along.</strong><br /></span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="8300421097395478162">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer2407387576186490440">One of those special books that, when you finish, you immediately want to find someone who hasn't read it, and press it into there hands, murmuring insistently, "<em>you <strong>have</strong> to read this!</em>"<br /><br />I'm a big Douglas Adams fan. The Hitchhikers Guide to the Galaxy is one of my all-time favourite series, and the Dirk Gently mysteries aren't far behind. When I set-up my Pantheon list of literary gods, Douglas Adams came straight in at Number 2 (behind Terry Pratchett) - and <em> <strong>Last Chance to See</strong> </em> was the one, key</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText2407387576186490440">One of those special books that, when you finish, you immediately want to find someone who hasn't read it, and press it into there hands, murmuring insistently, "<em>you <strong>have</strong> to read this!</em>"<br /><br />I'm a big Douglas Adams fan. The Hitchhikers Guide to the Galaxy is one of my all-time favourite series, and the Dirk Gently mysteries aren't far behind. When I set-up my Pantheon list of literary gods, Douglas Adams came straight in at Number 2 (behind Terry Pratchett) - and <em>
  <strong>Last Chance to See</strong>
</em> was the one, key book that I hadn't read of his.<br /><br />I found reading this a surprisingly emotional experience. I mean... Adams' voice is so strong in all his work, and within a couple of paragraphs I felt like I was back in his presence... a twelve year old boy sneaking the lamp back on after my parents had gone away, to read just one more chapter... and I felt that wave of grief wash over me again, just like the day I heard that he'd died. But... he's funny, and bright and grumpy and... just brilliant! I was sad to be reminded that he had passed away, but I was also hugely entertained and delighted that, despite being a non-fiction, this was every bit as good as his wildly imaginative speculative fiction.<br /><br />This is 30% ecology novel about endangered species, and 70% travel book about the adventures of a cranky, middle-aged Brit travelling to far-flung lands to visit said endangered species.<br /><br />The 30% is fascinating and the 70% kept me grinning. <br /><br />Some of the phrases have embedded themselves in my head... I will always think of rhinos as "nimble young volkswagens", and kakopos as the birds that have "forgotten that they've forgotten how to fly".<br /><br />One of those special books that, when you finish, you immediately want to find someone who hasn't read it, and press it into there hands, murmuring insistently, "<em>you <strong>have</strong> to read this!</em>"<br /><br />Even writing this review makes me want to go and re-read Dirk Gently...<br /><br /><strong>After this I read: </strong><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/326290863">Snow Crash</a></span>

<span id="freeTextContainer7518621317870162981">I'm not a zoology student but I have a 3 unit subject and we were required to visit either <em>Manila Zoo</em> or the <em>National Museum of the Philippines</em> animal research section, it was a part of our annual field trip without the teacher in charge. Because I'm new here in Manila last year my classmates decided to go both the zoo and the museum. In spite of the fact that the weather was hot, we were forced to go outside and take pictures for our journal because it was the last day of submission.<br /><br />Our first s</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText7518621317870162981">I'm not a zoology student but I have a 3 unit subject and we were required to visit either <em>Manila Zoo</em> or the <em>National Museum of the Philippines</em> animal research section, it was a part of our annual field trip without the teacher in charge. Because I'm new here in Manila last year my classmates decided to go both the zoo and the museum. In spite of the fact that the weather was hot, we were forced to go outside and take pictures for our journal because it was the last day of submission.<br /><br />Our first stop was the Museum, we were lucky that the researchers in charge the museum were present that time and they have the time to show their specialties. Every group was required to enter a certain room with a researcher in charge and they tackled about animals (of course) and plants. If I can still remember, only few have been discussed because of the limited materials present that time and the Museum is under construction by somewhat fail to finish.<br /><br />After that we went to <em>Manila Zoo</em>, the worst place in Manila. It was one of the dirtiest places here and not recommended to be visited every Sunday, although almost Filipino families wanted or already went here and have their Sunday picnic. You can also see animals that are suffering because of the lack of space and the grills that are not meant for them. They should be in the wild but they are needed to be preserve and be seen by the people. If you're an animal lover or somehow trying to be one soon, this is a great place to take your blowing mind and grab some board with quotes that are funny and in fact nobody cared to see you but only the lenses of cameras are interested in taking pictures of you and your ugly sign board.<br /><br />I'm not supporting <em>Manila Zoo</em>, but I think we must remember that there are always positive and negative outcome in every actions that we made or advantage and disadvantage why we made places like zoos or animal lab. In fact a specie is required to be hunt and be hunted so that other species must survive, we need balance in our ecosystem and we are just part of that small community who wanted and tried to survive for almost 5,000 years of existence. <br /><br />Douglas Adams and his coauthor, Mark Carwardine travels through greatness of God's creation. Looking for animals that are endangered in <em>Last Chance to See</em>. You don't need to become a knowledgeable zoologist, a 5 year naturalist or a tracker to enjoy the book that he dedicated to the people who wanted to see animals that are soon to be lost in books and in the memories of mankind. <br /><br />It was started when <strong>Douglas</strong> was assigned to write an article 3 years before the event to <em>Observer</em> and met <strong>Mark</strong>, a zoologist and both were assigned to look for an Aye-aye (<em>Daubentonia madagascariensis</em>), a rare specie of nocturnal Lemur which can be found only in Madagascar. After the unexpected meeting they decided to find more endangered animals and write a book for them to support the facilities and minor projects of the government in preserving those animals.<br /><br />They went through a very serious but hilarious journey and met nice researcher and people to guide us readers to explore the secret behind the thick forest of India, New Zealand, China and the famous Dodo's Island, Mauritius. Of course, before Douglas and his friend went to Mauritius, dodos (<em>Raphus cucullatus</em>) were already extinct in mid-17th century. But it is not too late for us to go outside and and discover new species.<br /><br /><img class="escapedImg" src="http://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1380345522i/700129.png" /><br /><em>True Colors, can't help but to laugh than to support them. If you see me naked and wearing blue paint you may laugh and take pictures of me and be posted it online. I don't know where they get their courage to model nude in public. *Sings True Colors</em><br /><br /><strong>Rating</strong> - <em>Last Chance to See</em> by Douglas Adams and Mark Carwardine, <strong>5 Sweets</strong> and the people who cared and want to see animals that are in near extinction. (It took me 3 pages of my book journal and unconquerable nights of thinking of those animals and asking myself, do I still have chance to see them before I die? My top 1 on my best book this year.     <div class="section">
      

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_1195082">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1195082" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/84023-j-g-keely" class="left imgcol" title="J.G. Keely"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1274333636p2/84023.jpg" alt="J.G. Keely" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/1195082?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Dec 14, 2012</a>

      <a href="/user/show/84023-j-g-keely" class="user" title="J.G. Keely">J.G. Keely</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it">liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/538321.Last_Chance_to_See" title="Hardcover 9780517582152" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/84023-j-g-keely?shelf=non-fiction" class="actionLinkLite">non-fiction</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/84023-j-g-keely?shelf=humor" class="actionLinkLite">humor</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/84023-j-g-keely?shelf=novel" class="actionLinkLite">novel</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/84023-j-g-keely?shelf=reviewed" class="actionLinkLite">reviewed</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/84023-j-g-keely?shelf=autobiographical" class="actionLinkLite">autobiographical</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/84023-j-g-keely?shelf=uk-and-ireland" class="actionLinkLite">uk-and-ireland</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer1195082">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9975791400383767969">Adams was an amazingly humorous fellow, but it can be easy to forget that the source of his humor is always surreal profundity. It's as if he sees a completely different world than the rest of us, but one which looks precisely the same. In this book (out-of-print when I found an editor's proof copy) Adams takes that hilariously disparate view and directs it like a spastic and noodly laser at the mis-management of our natural world. There is a reason that Richard Dawkins recalls Adams so fondly a</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText9975791400383767969">Adams was an amazingly humorous fellow, but it can be easy to forget that the source of his humor is always surreal profundity. It's as if he sees a completely different world than the rest of us, but one which looks precisely the same. In this book (out-of-print when I found an editor's proof copy) Adams takes that hilariously disparate view and directs it like a spastic and noodly laser at the mis-management of our natural world. There is a reason that Richard Dawkins recalls Adams so fondly as a compatriot in the fight for reason. Adams is as honest, sublime, and disarming as ever. <br /><br />I personally don't believe in a static view of nature. Extinction--even mass-extinction--has been a constant theme throughout prehistory. Humanity isn't even the first single species to cause the mass extinction of a huge variety of animals: algae did it millions of years before humans even existed.<br /><br />Animals compete for the same resources, and whenever there are changes in the environment, be they geographical or climatic, there are going to be extinctions as different species come into contact in new ways. Despite what a lot of badly-researched sci fi might tell you, evolution is not a process of improvement: no species is any more evolved than any other species, each species has simply evolved in different ways to meet the requirements of a different ecological niche.<br /><br />The coelecanth was a fish that first crawled out of the water hundreds of millions of years ago, and which we assumed had gone extinct until one was caught in 1975. That fish's descendents eventually produced the first lizards, which produced the first mammals, which produced the first primates, which eventually produced human beings. Yet, just because we evolved from the lowly coelecanth does not mean that we are 'more highly evolved'--stick a human being and a coelecanth in the middle of the ocean for a few days and it should be clear that we are just evolved to do different sorts of things.<br /><br />Part of the reason we're experiencing high rates of extinction right now is that there are more species now than at any other point, and a huge number of those species are extremely specialized to a certain type of lifestyle, meaning even a small adjustment in their environment is likely to drive them to extinction. Mr. Tibbles was a naughty cat: he hunted an entire species to extinction by himself. This was the Stephens Island Wren, a flightless bird which had evolved to live on nothing but the algae that accumulated on the rocky island.<br /><br />This is not evidence that Mr. Tibbles was more evolved than the wren, because Mr. Tibbles, left alone on the island, couldn't do what the wren did: survive off the island's resources. The reason cats, goats, rabbits, and pigs have been successful when introduced in new areas is because they are generalists, not specialists. They can survive in a wide variety of environments even when they are not the animal best-suited to that environment, because in times of change and upheaval, generalists outperform specialists.<br /><br />A group of scientists were testing the behavior of flies and discovered that if the flies entered an area and there was no food there, almost none of the flies would ever return to that area. Then, the scientists began to wait until the flies had checked an area, and then put food there after they left. Within a few generations, the flies who returned had been much more successful, and so their offspring predominated. Now nearly all the flies would return to the same areas, again and again.<br /><br />Yet, when the scientists reset the test to the original conditions, the specialized behavior died out, after only a few generations, because spending the time and energy and brain space on that behavior was just not worth it. It's the same reason that isolated bird populations tend to become flightless: flight is great for moving around and escaping enemies, but it takes a lot of energy to maintain, so if all you have to sustain you is algae, and there are no predators to flee, you might as well drop the showy flight thing and use those calories to keep your body warm and alive.<br /><br />One of the great benefits of this process to humans is that all of those horrible, terrifying treatment-resistant diseases we have produced by overuse (and misuse) of antibiotics are highly specialized, and so, if we just drastically reduce antibiotic use, normal, generalist strains of <em>e. coli</em> will drastically outperform specialist, antibiotic resistant strains and drive them out of the ecosystem, which is exactly what has happened in Scandinavia where antibiotic treatment reduction is already in place.<br /><br />No matter what humans do, we won't wipe out life, and we won't 'destroy the environment', we'll just change it. There are bacteria that live on radioactive rods in the middle of nuclear power plants, and on boiling, magma-fed vents at the lightless bottom of the sea, and there are even bacteria that can live in a sterile, sealed container eating nothing but solar radiation. Sure, we could change the environment so much that we would kill off all the large animals, including ourselves, and most plants, but something else will just survive and take over. The Chernobyl site is now one of the most lush and wild natural preserves in all of Russia.<br /><br />There is no single, static way for the world to be--the environment and the animals that live in it are always changing, and to some degree, humans complaining about the extinction of certain specialized animals is like an old person complaining that the world isn't 'like it used to be'. Just because the environment was the way it was when humans evolved, that doesn't mean it is the only way for the environment to be, or that it won't change, or that change is bad, or that we should or could stop that change.<br /><br />But we should ask whether we want to destroy ourselves, whether we want to set up an environmental system that favors superbacteria and destructively invasive species, because in the end, it's not about the world, it's about us and what we have to live with. The world will get along fine without us, after all.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="9975791400383767969">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review nosyndicate" id="review_932188096">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/932188096" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/7006637-santhosh" class="left imgcol" title="Santhosh"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1448012813p2/7006637.jpg" alt="Santhosh" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/932188096?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Jan 24, 2015</a>

      <a href="/user/show/7006637-santhosh" class="user" title="Santhosh">Santhosh</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/6950172-last-chance-to-see" title="Paperback 9780099536796" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/7006637-santhosh?shelf=favorites" class="actionLinkLite">favorites</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer932188096">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9005231834637410139"><em>We have not inherited the Earth from our ancestors, but have borrowed it from our children.</em><br />~ Anonymous<br /><br />Evolution is an ongoing process, and each species simply evolves in different ways to meet the requirements of its ecological niche, extremely specialized to that ecology and lifestyle. There is generally enough time, in the case of a natural change to its ecology, for the species to try to adapt and evolve further. To try to survive. Extinction is nothing new to Earth and 99.99% of all species</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText9005231834637410139"><em>We have not inherited the Earth from our ancestors, but have borrowed it from our children.</em><br />~ Anonymous<br /><br />Evolution is an ongoing process, and each species simply evolves in different ways to meet the requirements of its ecological niche, extremely specialized to that ecology and lifestyle. There is generally enough time, in the case of a natural change to its ecology, for the species to try to adapt and evolve further. To try to survive. Extinction is nothing new to Earth and 99.99% of all species that have ever lived are now nonextant. One species playing a role in completely wiping out another species is again something that's been happening since the days of algae. Homo sapiens, though, are unique in two aspects: 1) in being the only species to have the cognitive ability to identify and understand the micro- and macro-level impact and repercussions of their decisions and actions; and 2) in being the single largest factor in history to have the power to change an environment quite dramatically and suddenly, intentionally or otherwise, and positively or otherwise. And yet, the rate of extinction has increased exponentially in the last few decades and we're reviewing a book titled <em>Last Chance to See</em>.<br /><br />This book is as evocative, poignant and funny as some of Charlie Chaplin's best work. And that's not a comparison I'd ever make lightly. Light but with a heavy heart, the book drives home the affecting point that, with our anthropocentric view of Earth, we are slowly but surely driving a lot of species towards extinction, while also not exactly helping in the evolving of new species, thanks to our truly global footprint. Just imagine, an entire type of animal, gone forever with a zero percent probability of ever wishing them back. As the book sums up, <em>There is one last reason for caring, and I believe that no other is necessary. It is certainly the reason why so many people have devoted their lives to protecting the likes of rhinos, parakeets, kakapos and dolphins. And it is simply this: the world would be a poorer, darker, lonelier place without them</em>.<br /><br />The book also has some wonderful anecdotes and observations from the travels of Adams and Mark Carwardine, and can quite easily take a place just as a remarkable and entertaining travelogue. All with some wonderful, wry British humour laced with a tinge of witty profoundness. Bryson-esque, and with the context and content, maybe even better. An absolute delight.<br /><br />Travelogue + Evolution + Douglas Adams. 5 stars.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="9005231834637410139">...more</a>

          </span>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_3527235">

  <a href="/user/show/220791-lena" class="left imgcol" title="Lena"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1442474289p2/220791.jpg" alt="Lena" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/3527235?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Sep 28, 2008</a>

      <a href="/user/show/220791-lena" class="user" title="Lena">Lena</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>



        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/220791-lena?shelf=humor" class="actionLinkLite">humor</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/220791-lena?shelf=essays" class="actionLinkLite">essays</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer3527235">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer890765299374248736">Mark Carwardine was a zoologist working for the World Wildlife Fund when he was hired by a magazine to take Douglas Adams to see the world’s rarest nocturnal lemur, the Madagascar aye-aye. The trip was enough of a success that they decided having Adams write funny things about his attempt to visit endangered species was a good way to raise awareness about animal conservation, so they reunited a few years later to track down some other animals whose numbers have fallen into the double digits. The</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText890765299374248736">Mark Carwardine was a zoologist working for the World Wildlife Fund when he was hired by a magazine to take Douglas Adams to see the world’s rarest nocturnal lemur, the Madagascar aye-aye. The trip was enough of a success that they decided having Adams write funny things about his attempt to visit endangered species was a good way to raise awareness about animal conservation, so they reunited a few years later to track down some other animals whose numbers have fallen into the double digits. The resulting collection of ecology/travel essays is hands down one of my all time favorite books.<br /><br />During the course of their travels, Carwardine and Adams go to Indonesia to visit the Komodo dragon, Zaire to see the Northern White rhinoceros, New Zealand to see the Kakapo parrot, China to see the Yangtzee River dolphin and Mauritius to see the Rodrigues fruit bat. Adams’ style of absurdist humor is particularly well-suited to detailing the problems involved in merely getting to the places where these animals are supposed to be, since they are frequently located rather inconveniently in remote areas of third world countries. His front line reports set the stage by being laugh out loud funny, keeping us so entertained and open that by the time we finally do get to meet these precarious creatures, we have no choice but to care about them and their fates as much as our intrepid reporters do. <br /></span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="890765299374248736">...more</a>

        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer1057393884">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13716085639216304133">Have you ever read a book laughing so hard that tears are streaming down your face and then in the next five minutes, crying copiously and having very different tears stream down the same old face? Well, I just did. I always knew that the dodo was extinct but today I sat in a corner and wept for a full fifteen minutes because there are no dodos left in the world anymore. That is what the Last Chance to See does to you. It makes you see things about the world and what we've done with it, things y</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText13716085639216304133">Have you ever read a book laughing so hard that tears are streaming down your face and then in the next five minutes, crying copiously and having very different tears stream down the same old face? Well, I just did. I always knew that the dodo was extinct but today I sat in a corner and wept for a full fifteen minutes because there are no dodos left in the world anymore. That is what the Last Chance to See does to you. It makes you see things about the world and what we've done with it, things you always knew (but possibly were never interested in being aware of) in such a stark new light that it's like a whack to the back of your head. And to put across something as sensitive as this, in an entirely non-confrontational, non-preachy, poignant way laced with such uproarious humor, is something other writers can only dream of. Traversing across people, bureaucracy, rainforests, wildlife, flora and cultures with so much nonchalance, this one is an eye-opener and as cheesy as it sounds, could be your 'last chance' to see this world and your co-inhabitants with some new found love and respect. MUST READ.</span>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_249992146">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/249992146" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/4643358-dimitris-hall" class="left imgcol" title="Dimitris Hall"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1410023523p2/4643358.jpg" alt="Dimitris Hall" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/249992146?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Dec 31, 2011</a>

      <a href="/user/show/4643358-dimitris-hall" class="user" title="Dimitris Hall">Dimitris Hall</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/6950172-last-chance-to-see" title="Paperback 9780099536796" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer249992146">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10523488388746206718">Douglas Adams proved with this book that he wasn't just a brilliant science fiction writer with a virtually unrivalled wit and sense of humour; it went to show that he had an admirable, enviable even, sense of social and ecological responsibility, taking him, as far as I am concerned, from the "brilliant writer" tier, to the "paradigm of humanity" club, reserved only for those people (and there's not a lot of them around) that can work as <strong>true inspiration</strong> for me. Last Chance To See is a manifest</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText10523488388746206718">Douglas Adams proved with this book that he wasn't just a brilliant science fiction writer with a virtually unrivalled wit and sense of humour; it went to show that he had an admirable, enviable even, sense of social and ecological responsibility, taking him, as far as I am concerned, from the "brilliant writer" tier, to the "paradigm of humanity" club, reserved only for those people (and there's not a lot of them around) that can work as <strong>true inspiration</strong> for me. Last Chance To See is a manifesto on almost everything that's wrong or imbalanced in the world today -- and it was written more than 20 years ago. The Douglas Adams impish vibe that is so cherished by many serves as little more than a tasty side dish for this book. It is that good.<br /><br />My edition has a foreword by Richard Dawkins who has a similar opinion of the late man as I do. While I do not really agree with his flagship Atheist views (even if I would much sooner classify myself as an Atheist than a "Creationist"), he does do a magnificent job of summing up the point of this book in just a few words:<br /><br /><blockquote>Of the endangered animals that Douglas Adams and Mark Carwardine set out to see, one seems to have gone for good during the intervening two decades. We have noew lost our last chance to see the Yangtze river dolhpin. Or hear it, which is more to the point, for the river dolphin lived ina world where seeing was pretty much out of the question anyway: a murky, muddy river in which sonar came splendidly into its own -- until the arrival of massive noise pollution by boat engines.<br />The loss of the river dolphin is a tragedy, and some of the other wonderful characters in this book cannot be far behind. In his Last Word, Mark Carwardine reflects on why we should care when species, or shole major groups of animals and plants go extinct. He deals with the usual arguments:<br /><br /><blockquote>Every animal and plant is an integral part of its environment: even Komodo dragons have a major role to play in maintaining the ecological stability of their delicate island homes. If they disappear, so could many other species. And consercation is very much in time with our own survival. Animals and plants provide us with life-saving drugs and food, they pollinate crops and provide important ingredients for many industrial processes.</blockquote>
<br /><br />Yes, yes, he would say that kind of thing, it's expected of him. But the pity that we need to justify conservation on such human-centered, utilitarian grounds. To borrow an analogy I once used in a different context, it's a bit like justifying music on the grounds that it's good exercise for the violinist's right arm. Surely the real justification for saving these magnificent creatures is the one with which Mark rounds off the book, and which he obviously prefers:<br /><br /><blockquote>There is one last reason for caring, and I believe that no other is necessary. It is certainly the reason why so many people have devoted their lives to protecting the likes of rhinos, parakeets, kakapos and dolphins. And it is simply this: the world would be a poorer, darker, lonelier place without them.</blockquote>
<br /><br />[...]<br /><br />He [Douglas Adams] saw with his own eyes how quickly such painstaking edifices of evolutionary artifice can be torn down and tossed to oblivion. He tried to do something about it. So should we, if only to honour the memory of this unrepeatable specimen of Homo Sapiens. For once, the specific name is well deserved.</blockquote><br /><br />My respect also goes to Mark Carwardine, who has continued to bring the word out all these years, as well as to all the people all over the world, described in the book or not, that have devoted their lives to noble and moving ideals.<br /><br /><br /></span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="10523488388746206718">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_129903929">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/129903929" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/2593734-nicholas-armstrong" class="left imgcol" title="Nicholas Armstrong"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1284413844p2/2593734.jpg" alt="Nicholas Armstrong" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/129903929?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Nov 13, 2010</a>

      <a href="/user/show/2593734-nicholas-armstrong" class="user" title="Nicholas Armstrong">Nicholas Armstrong</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>



        


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer129903929">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3076456233976340378">If a book is well-written, and I don't simply mean it is an enjoyable romp, I mean <em>really</em> well written -- it is consistent, there is no break in the flow, voice, or tone -- and it accomplishes the elusive task of making you think, then what might we call that book? I would call it remarkable. <br /><br />Douglas Adams is one of the most amusing writers of all time, perhaps even the most amusing writer of all time; couple this with an incredible intellect and the ability to write quite well and you get a pre</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText3076456233976340378">If a book is well-written, and I don't simply mean it is an enjoyable romp, I mean <em>really</em> well written -- it is consistent, there is no break in the flow, voice, or tone -- and it accomplishes the elusive task of making you think, then what might we call that book? I would call it remarkable. <br /><br />Douglas Adams is one of the most amusing writers of all time, perhaps even the most amusing writer of all time; couple this with an incredible intellect and the ability to write quite well and you get a pretty impressive figure. Now take that figure and have him discuss and explore complicated, real issues and see what comes up.<br /><br />The beauty of this book isn't, as you might believe, what it says about rare species. Well, maybe somewhat, but it is the fact that it so clearly elucidates so much about these species, especially their environments. For example, there was not as much to say about the white rhino as many of the other species, but there was quite a lot to say about Zaire. <em>This</em> is what made the book so amazing; it wasn't just about the animals, it was about the ways in which people are fighting to protect them, the crap they have to wade through to do it and the ridiculous governments that have hold of some of these animals. It is, essentially, a book about the world and what we are doing to it and to each other.<br /><br />I want to point out something else as well. I love animals, and that was a prime motivator for reading this book, but I don't think one has to love them to appreciate this book, especially considering the fact that I am now questioning the wisdom of protecting some of these animals, which was the reverse of the intention. My point is simply that this is about far more than animals; it is about us. It is about life, and even, at some points, about religion.<br /><br />This is a remarkably funny and witty book and I feel that anyone who wants to really look at their own world needs to read it.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="3076456233976340378">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer2106513476263572502">My husband urged me to read this book when it was first written but I feared it would be too depressing. Then when Stephen Fry and Mark Carwardine retraced the steps of Carwardine and Adams on TV I realised what a great story this was. Still it languished unopened on our shelves. One day after Christmas it finally caught my eye and said...read me now before it's too late. A little parable for animals and plants in obscure places....appreciate and notice and cherish me now...... Before it's too l</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText2106513476263572502">My husband urged me to read this book when it was first written but I feared it would be too depressing. Then when Stephen Fry and Mark Carwardine retraced the steps of Carwardine and Adams on TV I realised what a great story this was. Still it languished unopened on our shelves. One day after Christmas it finally caught my eye and said...read me now before it's too late. A little parable for animals and plants in obscure places....appreciate and notice and cherish me now...... Before it's too late....<br /><br />Like the Yangtze River dolphin Douglas Adams is no more but the book celebrates the dedication of those all over the world who are fighting, sometimes against enormous odds to fight for the right of indigenous species to live long and prosper. We need to notice more, care more, do more.... </span>

<span id="freeTextContainer10610513432528632150">Douglas Adams. Author of the Hitchhikers' Guide to the Galaxy. Writing a nature book? Huh?<br /><br />I suppose I shouldn't be surprised, given that this is Douglas Adams, but Last Chance to See is an excellent and thought provoking book, which is part laugh-out-loud travelogue (including an interesting reminder of what China was like just 25years ago), and part conservation plea<br /><br />It's power as a book lies is in the juxtaposition of the sharp observations and comic stories of his travels with Mark Carwardinea</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText10610513432528632150">Douglas Adams. Author of the Hitchhikers' Guide to the Galaxy. Writing a nature book? Huh?<br /><br />I suppose I shouldn't be surprised, given that this is Douglas Adams, but Last Chance to See is an excellent and thought provoking book, which is part laugh-out-loud travelogue (including an interesting reminder of what China was like just 25years ago), and part conservation plea<br /><br />It's power as a book lies is in the juxtaposition of the sharp observations and comic stories of his travels with Mark Carwardineand the plight of the animals it describes. Sadly the Yangtse River Dolphin, subject of one of the chapters, has now been declared functionally, and perhaps actually, extinct; with the Northern White Rhino about to follow it into history.<br /><br />As he says towards the end of the book "It's easy to think that as a result of the extinction of the dodo we are now sadder and wiser, but there's a lot of evidence to suggest that we are merely sadder and better informed."<br /><br />When are we going to learn?<br /><br /></span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="10610513432528632150">...more</a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/1444701960?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Nov 21, 2015</a>

      <a href="/user/show/13908931-ariel-mot-avant" class="user" title="Ariel (mot_avant)">Ariel (mot_avant)</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>



        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/13908931-ariel-mot-avant?shelf=read-in-2015" class="actionLinkLite">read-in-2015</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer1444701960">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13168303285380879355">In 1985 Douglas Adams and Mark Carwardine set off in the hope of spotting the Madagascar aye-aye, a nocturnal lemur nearing extinction. The trip was a success and so the duo came back together a couple years later to seek out more animals that were verging on the brink with the idea that their travels and Adams' writing would shine a much needed spotlight on said brink. <br /><br />Like the Madagascar aye aye, my encounter with Adams' <em>Last Chance to See</em> adventuring was a nocturnal one. In simplicity, I coul</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText13168303285380879355">In 1985 Douglas Adams and Mark Carwardine set off in the hope of spotting the Madagascar aye-aye, a nocturnal lemur nearing extinction. The trip was a success and so the duo came back together a couple years later to seek out more animals that were verging on the brink with the idea that their travels and Adams' writing would shine a much needed spotlight on said brink. <br /><br />Like the Madagascar aye aye, my encounter with Adams' <em>Last Chance to See</em> adventuring was a nocturnal one. In simplicity, I couldn't put it down. The spotlight shone in Adams' humor and intellect, both fleshing out the weight of their experience. That it mattered to him, moved him. <br /><br />I could go on about Douglas-Adams-as-a-synonym-for-brilliancy but it's been done. What I will say is that I love reading Adams because he seems to have been gifted with the rare ability to see the world from a slightly removed angle than the rest and the even rarer ability to translate such a view to those of us unaware. <br /><br />This is an important book; a swollen, dog-eared, in peril of a broken spine book. A pass-it-on book.<br /></span>

          </span>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review nosyndicate" id="review_155230727">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/155230727" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/1376766-becky" class="left imgcol" title="Becky"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1446571694p2/1376766.jpg" alt="Becky" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/155230727?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Dec 16, 2015</a>

      <a href="/user/show/1376766-becky" class="user" title="Becky">Becky</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/823526.Last_chance_to_see_" title="Paperback 9780330320023" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/1376766-becky?shelf=humorous" class="actionLinkLite">humorous</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/1376766-becky?shelf=non-fiction" class="actionLinkLite">non-fiction</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/1376766-becky?shelf=reviewed" class="actionLinkLite">reviewed</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/1376766-becky?shelf=audiobook" class="actionLinkLite">audiobook</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/1376766-becky?shelf=i-want-this" class="actionLinkLite">i-want-this</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/1376766-becky?shelf=highly-recommended" class="actionLinkLite">highly-recommended</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/1376766-becky?shelf=2011" class="actionLinkLite">2011</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer155230727">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12166452936439194128">Last Chance to See chronicles Douglas Adams and Mark Carwardine's trips to far flung places to see, and call attention to, endangered and borderline extinct animal species. <br /><br />I listened to the audio, read by Douglas himself (except for the very end which is read by Mark Carwardine), and it was brilliant. Not only does Douglas really bring each destination and trip to life, but he does so in a way that allows the reader to understand his feelings regarding these things, but without sounding judgmen</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText12166452936439194128">Last Chance to See chronicles Douglas Adams and Mark Carwardine's trips to far flung places to see, and call attention to, endangered and borderline extinct animal species. <br /><br />I listened to the audio, read by Douglas himself (except for the very end which is read by Mark Carwardine), and it was brilliant. Not only does Douglas really bring each destination and trip to life, but he does so in a way that allows the reader to understand his feelings regarding these things, but without sounding judgmental or like a finger-wagging nag. <br /><br />And on top of that, his sense of humor and brilliant mind are like sunshine on a mostly cloudy day. It's all seriousseriousserious... then WHAM! Unexpected funny. What I wouldn't have given to be tagging along for this trip! DA, exotic locations, trying to help endangered animals, and my goodness, if the experts that they talked to were all half as funny and awesome as DA portrayed them, I'd have needed some adult diapers so that when I pissed myself laughing nobody would know and I could keep on trekkin'. As it was, just listening to him imitate them, I kept giggling uncontrollably. Just imagine that with facial expressions. Oh man. <br /><br />But this is really a serious book. It's a bit dated now, from the late 80s or so, but the issues it contains are just as prevalent now as they were then, if not more so. Some of the plights he described, particularly that of the Yangtze River Dolphin, the Baiji. Evolutionarily blind because of the muddy river water, they existed for thousands of years by echolocation, and then when China started to develop industrially and started to use the river with powered boats etc, the dolphins could no longer hear, became confused and would be hit by boats or propellers, or get caught in fishing nets and die either way. And that's not to mention the pollution and poisons in the water from industrial waste, or the fact that people still eat them. Or did, before they were declared "functionally extinct" in 2006. *sigh* Just the way that DA described these poor dolphins broke my heart - blind, confused, hungry due to lack of food due to human fishermen, in constant fear and pain from the pollution and noise pollution in the water, and with nowhere to go... it makes me really hate humanity sometimes. But, in the Chinese people's defense, it seems that they truly didn't know that there were dolphins in the river until almost too late, and then they scrambled to help... But too late won. :(<br /><br />I definitely recommend this book. It's an important and brilliant one. And it's Douglas Adams. That should be enough.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="12166452936439194128">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer7891289977827395676">UPDATED!<br /><br />Douglas Adams' famous book about his travels to animals that are about to be extinct. As entertaining, hilarious and smart as everything else Adams has written, but because of its subject definitely my favourite of his books. I can't believe I waited so long to read this. Maybe, if more people had read this book sooner, the statistics would look a bit better today.   <span style="display:none" id="freeText7891289977827395676">UPDATED!<br /><br />Douglas Adams' famous book about his travels to animals that are about to be extinct. As entertaining, hilarious and smart as everything else Adams has written, but because of its subject definitely my favourite of his books. I can't believe I waited so long to read this. Maybe, if more people had read this book sooner, the statistics would look a bit better today. Let's take a look at how the animals that Adams visited in 1990 are doing 16 years later.<br /><br />Komodo Dragon <br />1990: appr. 5000<br />2006: appr. 6000<br />Classified Vulnerable by the IUCN Red List, mostly due to its restricted habitat.<br /><br />Mountain Gorilla <br />1990: ca. 280<br />2006: ca. 380<br /><strong>2010</strong>: About 300 (Source: IUCN).<br />Classified Critically Endangered by the IUCN.<br /><br />Northern White Rhinoceros<br />1990: 22<br />2006: 5 to 20, depending on source<br />2010: 8, worldwide, including zoo animals (Source: <a rel="nofollow" href="http://www.northernwhiterhinolastchance.com/" target="_blank">http://www.northernwhiterhinolastchan...</a>), or maybe 4 (source IUCN).<br />Classified Critically Endangered.<br /><br />Kakapo<br />1990: 40<br />2006: 86<br /><strong>2010</strong>: 122 (Source: Wikipedia)<br />Classified Critically Endangered.<br /><br />Baiji Dolphin <br />1990: 200<br />2006: unknown. 1998 7 were found<br /><strong>2010</strong>: Last sighted in August 2007 (not proven). So I guess it's safe to say that the Baiji is extinct, and, like Adams said, it was the last chance to see one. We sure won't get to see one anymore. Ever.<br /><br />Classified Critically Endangered, possibly extinct. Which, if you haven't guessed already, is the last step to extinct. (Source: IUCN)<br /><br />Mauritius Kestrel<br />1990: I can't seem to find the information in the book.<br />2006: ca. 1000<br /><strong>2010: ca. 1000, trend increasing (Source: IUCN)<br />Classified Vulnerable<br /><br />Echo Parakeet <br />1990: 15<br />2006: less than 200<br />Classified Critically Endangered.<br /><strong>2010</strong> Endangered, trend increasing! Yay for the Parakeet, it got downlisted by the IUCN!<br /><br />Cafe Marron/Ramus Mania:<br />The one plant he visited, the wild coffee plant Ramus Mania, seems to have disappeared, if not from Mauritius, at least from the web.<br /><br /><strong>UPDATE in 2010</strong>: Here's more information about the coffee plant: <a rel="nofollow" href="http://insel-rodrigues.blogspot.com/2009/08/cafe-marron-little-coffee-plant-that.html" target="_blank">http://insel-rodrigues.blogspot.com/2...</a></strong></span>

        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer1475769864">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer18321072215712479924">I received this book in a book exchange, and I was excited because I had read all of Adams' Hitchhikers Guide to the Galaxy books. But this book was different. It was a nonfiction journey of a few men traveling to glimpse and understand rare species of endangered animals. <br /><br />And honestly? I was delighted to read it. <br /><br />The whole book seemed to echo one of my truest life philosophies: we should all be living our most awesome lives doing weirdly awesome things. <br /><br />That's just what these guys did. </span>

          </span>
        </div>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_80961043">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/80961043" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/2793431-lance-greenfield" class="left imgcol" title="Lance Greenfield"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1442658682p2/2793431.jpg" alt="Lance Greenfield" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/80961043?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Jan 13, 2015</a>

      <a href="/user/show/2793431-lance-greenfield" class="user" title="Lance Greenfield">Lance Greenfield</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>



        


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer80961043">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7524365558078339731">Last Chance to See is a wonderful book by the late Douglas Adams. It is not Science Fiction. It is his account of his travels around the globe to observe some of the planet's most endangered species. It also contains liberal smatterings of his customary humour. He also shows us his great insight and compassion. Such a shame that he moved on before any of the creatures that he tells us about in this book.</span>

          </span>
        </div>


      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">

      <a href="/user/show/4777235-peggy" class="user" title="Peggy">Peggy</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer704727260">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8444286639665648546">I love this book; possibly more than I love the Hitchhiker Series. Last Chance to See is a secret treasure and more people should know about it. Adams uses his gentle wit to shine a light on what the world is in danger of losing – birds and bats and dolphins. If you want to see a clever writer use his powers for good, pick up Last Chance to See. You won’t regret it. </span>

          </span>
        </div>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_1350600743">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1350600743" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/58498-brook" class="left imgcol" title="brook"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1334606551p2/58498.jpg" alt="brook" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/1350600743?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Aug 13, 2015</a>

      <a href="/user/show/58498-brook" class="user" title="brook">brook</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/18413020-last-chance-to-see" title="ebook 9781299040564" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer1350600743">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12975561301552019391">Before I read this, I saw that another reviewer said "Douglas Adams was wasted on science fiction." I considered this almost blasphemous, and had pitchforks leaned against the doorjamb, until I read <em>Last Chance to See</em>. Now it's merely more praise. <br /><br />I had been told "if you like Adams, you have to read this." Well, no, I don't, as I don't want to see someone I admire struggle somewhere else. I don't want to see George Foreman selling grills. I don't want to watch Jerry Rice on <em>Dancing With The Star</em></span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText12975561301552019391">Before I read this, I saw that another reviewer said "Douglas Adams was wasted on science fiction." I considered this almost blasphemous, and had pitchforks leaned against the doorjamb, until I read <em>Last Chance to See</em>. Now it's merely more praise. <br /><br />I had been told "if you like Adams, you have to read this." Well, no, I don't, as I don't want to see someone I admire struggle somewhere else. I don't want to see George Foreman selling grills. I don't want to watch Jerry Rice on <em>Dancing With The Stars</em>. I don't want to watch Vonnegut fail completely as a father in real life.<br /><br />Nothing he writes will ever take the place of <em>HHGG</em>. However, as nature/wildlife writers go, this guy is good. I would even - please do not lynch me for this - allow him to forego writing HHGG if it meant we got a similarly-sized body of naturalist literature. The voice is exactly the same as in HHGG, but here the protagonists are the author and his traveling companion. If you care nothing for endangered (some now extinct, 20 years later) species, you will still get a laugh out of the book. The scene at the airport in Africa (Zaire? Zimbabwe?) with the two waiting rooms is just classic. Hell, the whole thing is classic. And the ability of Adams to have the reader relate to *everything* in the story, from his character, the locals, the animals, even the damned islands, is wonderful. <br /><br />I wish, now, that there was a similarly incorrectly-labeled "trilogy" of these books to put on my shelf, as there is with HHGG. Damned shame. </span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="12975561301552019391">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1182922165" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/11271247-jessie" class="left imgcol" title="Jessie"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1424055697p2/11271247.jpg" alt="Jessie" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/1182922165?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Mar 02, 2015</a>

      <a href="/user/show/11271247-jessie" class="user" title="Jessie">Jessie</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>



        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/11271247-jessie?shelf=2015" class="actionLinkLite">2015</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer1182922165">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9772504139179090122">Oh Douglas! Such a wonderful man, such a superb writer. Desperately did I wish that I was with him as he worked his way through foreign wildernesses. Yet, I can't help but feel that his descriptions are even more real then reality would have been. Adams has (had :( ) such an uncanny knack of seeing what is not there, and of identifying what is. Of being able to distinguish the complexities of the human psyche and completely make a mockery of them......but in an inspiring way? His ability to see</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText9772504139179090122">Oh Douglas! Such a wonderful man, such a superb writer. Desperately did I wish that I was with him as he worked his way through foreign wildernesses. Yet, I can't help but feel that his descriptions are even more real then reality would have been. Adams has (had :( ) such an uncanny knack of seeing what is not there, and of identifying what is. Of being able to distinguish the complexities of the human psyche and completely make a mockery of them......but in an inspiring way? His ability to see the humour in any situation, yet draw you into the severity of it at the same time, is a talent possessed by very few.<br /><br />There is but one issue I have with an otherwise magical, depressingly inspiring, eye opening account of a life changing experience (that is exploring the areas where species are being threatened by human interference), and it is a minor one. Perhaps even a silly one. And that is the overuse of the words 'malign' and 'benign'. I think he used it to describe the same thing about six times within one paragraph once. Unfortunately I found it somewhat tedious. Oh! And a times, when I was a little tired, his continuous sentences, where his pen could quite obviously not keep up with his brain, were a little confusing. But when refreshed, these sentences were rather endearing as you could feel the excitement or outrage bubbling up through the pages. </span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="9772504139179090122">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer904510933107584301">Nobody writes like Douglas Adams writes. He's one of the few guys that can have me laughing uproariously while also pondering something deep and important. Or pondering the sad legacy of humans while laughing. Or shaking my head at the sheer stupidity of humans.<br /><br />That being said, while there are many funny moments in this book, overall, it's heartbreaking book to read. In anyone else's hands, I believe it would have been pleadingly maudlin. <br /><br />With Adams, instead, it's hopeful.<br /><br />Nobody writes like Doug</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText904510933107584301">Nobody writes like Douglas Adams writes. He's one of the few guys that can have me laughing uproariously while also pondering something deep and important. Or pondering the sad legacy of humans while laughing. Or shaking my head at the sheer stupidity of humans.<br /><br />That being said, while there are many funny moments in this book, overall, it's heartbreaking book to read. In anyone else's hands, I believe it would have been pleadingly maudlin. <br /><br />With Adams, instead, it's hopeful.<br /><br />Nobody writes like Douglas Adams writes. I miss him.</span>

    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">

      <a href="/user/show/10415843-jennifer" class="user" title="Jennifer">Jennifer</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it">liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span></span>



        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/10415843-jennifer?shelf=bookends-book-club" class="actionLinkLite">bookends-book-club</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/10415843-jennifer?shelf=2015-reads" class="actionLinkLite">2015-reads</a>
